		</ul>
		
	</div>

					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Food made of flour and water</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Bread_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Bread (disambiguation)">Bread (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316064257">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent;color:inherit}.mw-parser-output td{padding-left:inherit;padding-right:inherit}}</style><table class="infobox hrecipe adr"><caption class="infobox-title fn"><span>Bread</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-caption" style="padding-bottom:0.25em;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;">Various <a href="/wiki/Bread_roll" title="Bread roll">leavened breads</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316373843">@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .sister-inline-image img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .sister-inline-image img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}</style><span class="noviewer sister-inline-image" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/20px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a></span> <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Bread" class="extiw" title="b:Cookbook:Bread">Cookbook: Bread</a></li><li>&#160;<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/20px-Commons-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="12" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/40px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1376" /></a></span> <a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Bread" class="extiw" title="commons:Bread">Media&#58; Bread</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Bread</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Baking" title="Baking">baked</a> food product made from water, <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a>, and often <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> across the world, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. Throughout <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a> and around the world, it has been an important part of many cultures' diets. It is one of the oldest human-made foods, having been of significance since the dawn of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, and plays an essential role in both religious rituals and <a href="/wiki/Secular" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular">secular</a> culture.
</p><p>Bread may be <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavened</a> by naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Microbes" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbes">microbes</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">sourdough</a>), chemicals (e.g. baking soda), industrially produced <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker&#39;s yeast">yeast</a>, or high-pressure aeration, which creates the gas bubbles that fluff up bread. Bread may also be <a href="/wiki/Unleavened_bread" title="Unleavened bread">unleavened</a>. In many countries, mass-produced bread often contains <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">additives</a> to improve flavor, texture, color, shelf life, nutrition, and ease of production.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2></div>
<p>The word <i>bread</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> and several other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> first used in English around year 1200.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-oed&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its meaning was <i>bit, crumb, or morsel</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-oed&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> name was <i>brēotan</i> or <i>brēad</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-UOM01022026&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UOM01022026-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_bread" title="History of bread">History of bread</a></div>
<p>Bread is one of the oldest prepared foods.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Evidence from 30,000 years ago in Europe and Australia revealed starch residue on rocks used for pounding plants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is possible that during this time, starch extract from the roots of plants, such as <a href="/wiki/Cattails" class="mw-redirect" title="Cattails">cattails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferns" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferns">ferns</a>, was spread on a flat rock, placed over a fire and cooked into a primitive form of <a href="/wiki/Flatbread" title="Flatbread">flatbread</a>. The oldest evidence of bread-making has been found in a 14,500-year-old <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian</a> site in Jordan's northeastern desert.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Around 10,000 BC, with the dawn of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> age and the spread of agriculture, grains became the mainstay of making bread. Yeast spores are ubiquitous, including on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal grains</a>, so any dough left to rest leavens naturally.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman_baking_bread-E_25213-IMG_4514-gradient.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Woman_baking_bread-E_25213-IMG_4514-gradient.jpg/250px-Woman_baking_bread-E_25213-IMG_4514-gradient.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Woman_baking_bread-E_25213-IMG_4514-gradient.jpg/500px-Woman_baking_bread-E_25213-IMG_4514-gradient.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="4500" /></a><figcaption><i>Woman baking bread</i> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;2200 BC</span>); <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>An early leavened bread was baked as early as 6000 BC in southern Mesopotamia, cradle of the <a href="/wiki/Sumerians" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumerians">Sumerian</a> civilization, who may have passed on the knowledge to the Egyptians around 3000 BC. The Egyptians refined the process and started adding <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a>. The Sumerians were already using <a href="/wiki/Ash" title="Ash">ash</a> to supplement the dough as it was baked.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-arzani11&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arzani11-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There were multiple sources of <a href="/wiki/Leavening" class="mw-redirect" title="Leavening">leavening</a> available for early bread. Airborne yeasts could be harnessed by leaving uncooked dough exposed to air for some time before cooking. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> reported that the <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iberians" title="Iberians">Iberians</a> used the foam skimmed from <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Barm" title="Barm">barm</a>, to produce "a lighter kind of bread than other peoples" such as <a href="/wiki/Barm_cake" title="Barm cake">barm cake</a>. Parts of the ancient world that drank wine instead of beer used a paste composed of <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a> juice and flour that was allowed to begin fermenting, or wheat bran steeped in <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>, as a source for <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>. The most common source of leavening was to retain a piece of dough from the previous day to use as a form of sourdough <a href="/wiki/Bread_starter" class="mw-redirect" title="Bread starter">starter</a>, as Pliny also reported.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> all considered the degree of refinement in the bakery arts as a sign of civilization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-arzani11&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arzani11-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chorleywood_bread_process" title="Chorleywood bread process">Chorleywood bread process</a> was developed in 1961; it uses the intense mechanical working of dough to dramatically reduce the <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> period and the time taken to produce a loaf. The process, whose high-energy mixing allows for the use of grain with a lower protein content, is now widely used around the world in large factories. As a result, bread can be produced very quickly and at low costs to the manufacturer and the consumer. However, there has been some criticism of the effect on nutritional value.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Types">Types</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_breads" title="List of breads">List of breads</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti style="width:342px;max-width:342px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:131px;max-width:131px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:96px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">Dark <a href="/wiki/Sprouted_bread" title="Sprouted bread">sprouted bread</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:106px;max-width:106px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:96px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg/120px-Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg" decoding="async" width="104" height="97" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg/250px-Reik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4_view_from_above.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1465" data-file-height="1365" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Ruisreik%C3%A4leip%C3%A4" title="Ruisreikäleipä">Ruisreikäleipä</a>, a flat <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a> flour loaf with a hole</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Bread is the <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and in European-derived cultures such as those in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>. This is in contrast to parts of South and East Asia, where <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Noodles" class="mw-redirect" title="Noodles">noodles</a> are the staple. Bread is usually made from a <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>-<a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">flour</a> <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a> that is cultured with yeast, allowed to rise, and baked in an <a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">oven</a>. Carbon dioxide and ethanol vapors produced during yeast fermentation result in bread's air pockets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>   Owing to its high levels of <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a> (which give the dough sponginess and elasticity), <a href="/wiki/Common_wheat" title="Common wheat">common or bread wheat</a> is the most common grain used for the preparation of bread, which makes the largest single contribution to the world's food supply of any food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAO&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bread is also made from the flour of other wheat species (including <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Einkorn" title="Einkorn">einkorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamut" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamut">kamut</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Non-wheat cereals including <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">rye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (corn), <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">oats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> have been used to make bread, but, with the exception of rye, usually in combination with wheat flour as they have less gluten.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gluten-free_diet" title="Gluten-free diet">Gluten-free</a> breads are made using flours from a variety of ingredients such as almonds, rice, sorghum, corn, legumes such as beans, and tubers such as cassava. Since these foods lack gluten, dough made from them may not hold its shape as the loaves rise, and their crumb may be dense with little aeration. Additives such as <a href="/wiki/Xanthan_gum" title="Xanthan gum">xanthan gum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guar_gum" title="Guar gum">guar gum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydroxypropyl_methylcellulose" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroxypropyl methylcellulose">hydroxypropyl methylcellulose</a> (HPMC), <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Egg_as_food" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg as food">eggs</a> are used to compensate for the lack of gluten.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-LamacchiaCamarca2014&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LamacchiaCamarca2014-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (October 2025)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-VoltaCaio2015Quotation&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VoltaCaio2015Quotation-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MulderWanrooijQuotation&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MulderWanrooijQuotation-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-HischenhuberCrevelQuotation&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HischenhuberCrevelQuotation-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 242px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Sangak_17.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Sangak, an Iranian flatbread"><img alt="Sangak, an Iranian flatbread" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Sangak_17.jpg/500px-Sangak_17.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Sangak_17.jpg/960px-Sangak_17.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024" /></a></span></div>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Strucia — a type of European sweet bread"><img alt="Strucia — a type of European sweet bread" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg/500px-Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="160" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg/960px-Strucla_sweet_bread02.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a></span></div>

</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Properties">Properties</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Physical-chemical_composition">Physical-chemical composition</h3></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_phenol" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural phenol">phenolic</a> compounds are mainly found in <a href="/wiki/Hull_(botany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hull (botany)">hulls</a> in the form of insoluble bound <a href="/wiki/Ferulic_acid" title="Ferulic acid">ferulic acid</a>, where it is relevant to wheat resistance to fungal diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rye_bread" title="Rye bread">Rye bread</a> contains <a href="/wiki/Phenolic_acid" title="Phenolic acid">phenolic acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferulic_acid_dehydrodimer" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferulic acid dehydrodimer">ferulic acid dehydrodimers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Three <a href="/wiki/Natural_phenol" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural phenol">natural phenolic</a> glucosides, <a href="/wiki/Secoisolariciresinol_diglucoside" title="Secoisolariciresinol diglucoside">secoisolariciresinol diglucoside</a>, <a href="/wiki/P-coumaric_acid_glucoside" class="mw-redirect" title="P-coumaric acid glucoside">p-coumaric acid glucoside</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ferulic_acid_glucoside&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ferulic acid glucoside (page does not exist)">ferulic acid glucoside</a>, can be found in commercial breads containing <a href="/wiki/Flaxseed" class="mw-redirect" title="Flaxseed">flaxseed</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg/250px-Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg/330px-Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg/500px-Small_home_made_bread_with_pumpkin_and_sunflower_seeds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a><figcaption>Small homemade bread with <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin_seed" title="Pumpkin seed">pumpkin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_seed" title="Sunflower seed">sunflower seeds</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Glutenin" title="Glutenin">Glutenin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gliadin" title="Gliadin">gliadin</a> are functional proteins found in wheat bread that contribute to the structure of bread. Glutenin forms interconnected gluten networks within bread through interchain <a href="/wiki/Disulfide" title="Disulfide">disulfide</a> bonds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wieser&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieser-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Gliadin binds weakly to the gluten network established by glutenin via intrachain disulfide bonds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wieser&#95;26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wieser-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Structurally, bread can be defined as an elastic-plastic <a href="/wiki/Foam" title="Foam">foam</a> (same as <a href="/wiki/Expanded_polystyrene" class="mw-redirect" title="Expanded polystyrene">styrofoam</a>). The glutenin protein contributes to its <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elastic</a> nature, as it is able to regain its initial shape after deformation. The gliadin protein contributes to its <a href="/wiki/Plasticity_(physics)" title="Plasticity (physics)">plastic</a> nature, because it demonstrates non-reversible structural change after a certain amount of applied force. Because air pockets within this gluten network result from carbon dioxide production during leavening, bread can be defined as a foam, or a gas-in-solid solution.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Acrylamide" title="Acrylamide">Acrylamide</a>, like in other starchy foods that have been heated higher than 120&#160;°C (248&#160;°F), has been found in recent years to occur in bread. Acrylamide is <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxicity" title="Neurotoxicity">neurotoxic</a>, has adverse effects on male reproduction and developmental toxicity and is <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogenic</a>. A study has found that more than 99 percent of the acrylamide in bread is found in the crust.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A study by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hohenheim" title="University of Hohenheim">University of Hohenheim</a> found that industrially produced bread typically has a high proportion of <a href="/wiki/FODMAP" title="FODMAP">FODMAP</a> carbohydrates due to a short rising time (often only one hour). The high proportion of FODMAP carbohydrates in such bread then causes <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">flatulence</a>. This is particularly problematic in intestinal diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Irritable_bowel_syndrome" title="Irritable bowel syndrome">irritable bowel syndrome</a>. While in traditional bread making the dough rises for several hours, industrial breads rise for a much shorter time, usually only one hour. However, a sufficiently long rising time is important to break down the indigestible FODMAP carbohydrates. Some flours (for example, <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" class="mw-redirect" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn</a>) contain fewer FODMAPs, but the difference between grain types is relatively small (between 1 and 2 <a href="/wiki/Weight_percent" class="mw-redirect" title="Weight percent">percent by weight</a>). Instead, 90% of the FODMAPs that cause discomfort can be broken down during a rising time of four hours. In the study, whole-grain yeast doughs were examined after different rising times; the highest level of FODMAPs was present after one hour in each case and decreased thereafter. The study thus shows that it is essentially the baking technique and not the type of grain that determines whether a bread is well tolerated or not. A better tolerance of bread made from original cereals can therefore not be explained by the original cereal itself, but rather by the fact that traditional, artisanal baking techniques are generally used when baking original cereals, which include a long dough process. The study also showed that a long rising time also breaks down undesirable <a href="/wiki/Phytates" class="mw-redirect" title="Phytates">phytates</a> more effectively, flavors develop better, and the finished bread contains more biologically accessible <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Culinary_uses">Culinary uses</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG/250px-Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="179" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG/500px-Rew13c05-745a_Bread_Pudding.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="1945" data-file-height="1391" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Bread_pudding" title="Bread pudding">Bread pudding</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Bread can be served at many <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperatures</a>; once baked, it can subsequently be <a href="/wiki/Toast_(food)" title="Toast (food)">toasted</a>.  It is most commonly eaten with the hands, either by itself or as a carrier for other foods. Bread can be spread with <a href="/wiki/Butter" title="Butter">butter</a>, dipped into liquids such as <a href="/wiki/Gravy" title="Gravy">gravy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Soup" title="Soup">soup</a>;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it can be topped with various sweet and savory spreads, or used to make <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwiches</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meats</a>, cheeses, vegetables, and  <a href="/wiki/Condiments" class="mw-redirect" title="Condiments">condiments</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bread is used as an ingredient in other culinary preparations, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Breadcrumb" class="mw-redirect" title="Breadcrumb">breadcrumbs</a> to provide crunchy crusts or thicken sauces; toasted cubes of bread, called <a href="/wiki/Croutons" class="mw-redirect" title="Croutons">croutons</a>, are used as a salad topping; seasoned bread is used as <a href="/wiki/Stuffing" title="Stuffing">stuffing</a> inside roasted turkey; sweet or savoury <a href="/wiki/Bread_pudding" title="Bread pudding">bread puddings</a> are made with bread and various liquids; egg and milk-soaked bread is fried as <a href="/wiki/French_toast" title="French toast">French toast</a>; and bread is used as a binding agent in <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meatball" title="Meatball">meatballs</a> and other ground meat products.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nutritional_significance">Nutritional significance</h3></div>
<p>Bread is a good source of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> and micronutrients such as magnesium, iron, selenium, and B vitamins. Whole grain bread is a good source of dietary fiber and all breads are a common source of protein in the diet, though not a rich one.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Crust_and_crumb">Crust and crumb</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wei%C3%9Fbrot-1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Wei%C3%9Fbrot-1.jpg/250px-Wei%C3%9Fbrot-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Wei%C3%9Fbrot-1.jpg/500px-Wei%C3%9Fbrot-1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Bread with crust crack (half left at the top) and (lighter) crumb inside</figcaption></figure>
<p>The mass of bread consists of two primary components: the <i>crust</i> and <i>crumb</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wrigleyetal2015p404&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wrigleyetal2015p404-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><b>Bread crust</b> is formed from surface dough during the cooking process. It is hardened and browned through the <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a> using the sugars and amino acids due to the intense heat at the bread surface. The crust of most breads is harder, and more complexly and intensely flavored, than the rest. <a href="/wiki/Old_wives%27_tale" title="Old wives&#39; tale">Old wives' tales</a> suggest that eating the bread crust makes a person's hair curlier.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, the crust is rumored to be healthier than the remainder of the bread. Some studies have shown that this is true as the crust has more <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidants" class="mw-redirect" title="Antioxidants">antioxidants</a> such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pronyl&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pronyl (page does not exist)">pronyl</a>-<a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><b>Bread crumb</b> is the internal porous material consisting of bubbles with elastic walls. As the bread ages (becomes stale), the crumb becomes more firm.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wrigleyetal2015p404&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wrigleyetal2015p404-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Preparation">Preparation</h2></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Composition&#95;and&#95;chemistry"></span><span class="anchor" id="Chemistry&#95;and&#95;composition"></span>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg/250px-Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg/500px-Tortillas_de_rescoldo.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3194" /></a><figcaption>Steps in bread making, here for an unleavened Chilean tortilla</figcaption></figure>
<p>Doughs are usually <a href="/wiki/Baked" class="mw-redirect" title="Baked">baked</a>, but in some cuisines breads are <a href="/wiki/Steamed_bread" title="Steamed bread">steamed</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Mantou" title="Mantou">mantou</a>), fried (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Puri_(food)" title="Puri (food)">puri</a>), or baked on an unoiled <a href="/wiki/Frying_pan" title="Frying pan">frying pan</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Tortilla" title="Tortilla">tortillas</a>). It may be <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavened</a> or unleavened (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Matzah" title="Matzah">matzo</a>). <a href="/wiki/Edible_salt" class="mw-redirect" title="Edible salt">Salt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">leavening agents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Yeast_(baking)" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeast (baking)">yeast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baking_soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a> are common ingredients, though bread may contain other ingredients, such as <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (food)">egg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Raisin" title="Raisin">raisins</a>), <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">onion</a>), <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">nuts</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnut</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Poppy_seed" title="Poppy seed">poppy</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Methods of processing dough into bread include the <a href="/wiki/Straight_dough" title="Straight dough">straight dough process</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">sourdough process</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chorleywood_bread_process" title="Chorleywood bread process">Chorleywood bread process</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sponge_and_dough" title="Sponge and dough">sponge and dough process</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Formulation">Formulation</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg/250px-Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg/500px-Brot_-_Outubro_2013_-_Covalima.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption>Baking bread in <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" class="mw-redirect" title="East Timor">East Timor</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Professional bread recipes are stated using the <a href="/wiki/Baker_percentage" title="Baker percentage">baker's percentage</a> notation. The amount of flour is denoted to be 100%, and the other ingredients are expressed as a percentage of that amount by weight. Measurement by weight is more accurate and consistent than measurement by volume, particularly for dry ingredients. The proportion of water to flour is the most important measurement in a bread recipe, as it affects texture and crumb the most. Hard wheat flours <a href="/wiki/Farinograph#Method" title="Farinograph">absorb</a> about 62% <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, while softer wheat flours absorb about 56%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Common table breads made from these doughs result in a finely textured, light bread. Most <a href="/wiki/Artisan_bread" class="mw-redirect" title="Artisan bread">artisan bread</a> formulas contain anywhere from 60 to 75% water. In yeast breads, the higher water percentages result in more CO<sub>2</sub> bubbles and a coarser crumb.
</p><p>Dough recipes commonly call for 500 <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">grams</a> (about 1.1 pounds) of flour, which yields a single loaf of bread or two <a href="/wiki/Baguette" title="Baguette">baguettes</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Calcium_propanoate" title="Calcium propanoate">Calcium propanoate</a> is commonly added by commercial bakeries to retard the growth of molds.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Flour">Flour</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">Flour</a></div>
<p>Flour is grain ground into a powder. Flour provides the primary structure, starch and protein to the final baked bread. The <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> content of the flour is the best indicator of the quality of the bread <a href="/wiki/Dough" title="Dough">dough</a> and the finished bread. While bread can be made from all-purpose wheat flour, a specialty bread flour, containing more protein (12–14%), is recommended for high-quality bread. If one uses a flour with a lower protein content (9–11%) to produce bread, a shorter mixing time is required to develop gluten strength properly. An extended mixing time leads to oxidization of the dough, which gives the finished product a whiter crumb, instead of the cream color preferred by most artisan bakers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Wheat flour, in addition to its starch, contains three water-soluble protein groups (<a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Globulin" title="Globulin">globulin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proteose" title="Proteose">proteoses</a>) and two water-insoluble protein groups (<a href="/wiki/Glutenin" title="Glutenin">glutenin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gliadin" title="Gliadin">gliadin</a>). When flour is mixed with water, the water-soluble proteins dissolve, leaving the glutenin and gliadin to form the structure of the resulting bread. When relatively dry dough is worked by <a href="/wiki/Kneading" title="Kneading">kneading</a>, or wet dough is allowed to rise for a long time (see <a href="/wiki/No-knead_bread" title="No-knead bread">no-knead bread</a>), the glutenin forms strands of long, thin, chainlike molecules, while the shorter gliadin forms bridges between the strands of glutenin. The resulting networks of strands produced by these two proteins are known as <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>. Gluten development improves if the dough is allowed to <a href="/wiki/Autolyse" class="mw-redirect" title="Autolyse">autolyse</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Fortification">Fortification</h4></div>
<p>Processing of flours usually involves removal of the outer layers, which contain important nutrients. Such flours, and bread made from them, may be fortified by adding nutrients. Fortification with added calcium, iron, thiamine (Vitamin B1) and niacin (Vitamin B3) is a legal requirement in the UK (<a href="/wiki/Wholemeal_flour" class="mw-redirect" title="Wholemeal flour">wholemeal flours</a>, from which the nutrients have not been stripped, are exempt).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The unregulated term "wheatmeal" is used to describe flour containing some but not all of the outer covering and central part of the wheat grain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Liquids">Liquids</h3></div>
<p>Water, or some other liquid, is used to form the flour into a paste or dough. The weight or ratio of liquid required varies between recipes, but a ratio of three parts liquid to five parts flour is common for yeast breads.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recipes that use steam as the primary leavening method may have a liquid content in excess of one part liquid to one part flour. Instead of water, recipes may use liquids such as milk or other <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy products</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">buttermilk</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>), fruit juice, or eggs. These contribute additional sweeteners, fats, or leavening components, as well as water.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fats_or_shortenings">Fats or shortenings</h3></div>
<p>Fats, such as butter, vegetable oils, lard, or that contained in eggs, affect the development of gluten in breads by coating and lubricating the individual strands of protein. They also help to hold the structure together. If too much fat is included in a bread dough, the lubrication effect causes the protein structures to divide. A fat content of approximately 3% by weight is the concentration that produces the greatest leavening action.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition to their effects on leavening, fats also serve to tenderize breads and preserve freshness.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Bread_improvers">Bread improvers</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dough_conditioner" title="Dough conditioner">Dough conditioner</a></div>
<p>Bread improvers and dough conditioners are often used in producing commercial breads to reduce the time needed for rising and to improve texture and volume and to give <a href="/wiki/Staling" title="Staling">antistaling</a> effects. The substances used may be oxidising agents to strengthen the dough or reducing agents to develop gluten and reduce mixing time, emulsifiers to strengthen the dough or to provide other properties such as making slicing easier, or enzymes to increase gas production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tenbergen&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tenbergen-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Salt">Salt</h3></div>
<p>Salt (<a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>) is very often added to enhance flavor and restrict yeast activity. It also affects the crumb and the overall texture by stabilizing and strengthening<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the gluten. Some artisan bakers forego early addition of salt to the dough, whether wholemeal or refined, and wait until after a 20-minute rest to allow the dough to <a href="/wiki/Autolyse" class="mw-redirect" title="Autolyse">autolyse</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mixtures of salts are sometimes employed, such as employing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a> to reduce the sodium level, and <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">monosodium glutamate</a> to give flavor (<a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a>).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Leavening">Leavening</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Unleavened_bread" title="Unleavened bread">Unleavened bread</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg/250px-Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="187" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg/500px-Aberdour_Castle_-_Dough_Trough.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a><figcaption>A dough trough, located in <a href="/wiki/Aberdour_Castle" title="Aberdour Castle">Aberdour Castle</a>, once used for leavening bread</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leavening" class="mw-redirect" title="Leavening">Leavening</a> is the process of adding gas to a dough before or during baking to produce a lighter, more easily chewed bread. Most bread eaten in the West is leavened.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BAC&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chemicals">Chemicals</h3></div>
<p>A simple technique for leavening bread is the use of gas-producing chemicals.  There are two common methods. The first is to use <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Self-raising_flour" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-raising flour">self-raising flour</a> that includes baking powder.  The second is to include an acidic ingredient such as <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">buttermilk</a> and add <a href="/wiki/Baking_soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a>; the reaction of the acid with the soda produces gas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BAC&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chemically leavened breads are called <i><a href="/wiki/Quick_bread" title="Quick bread">quick breads</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Soda_bread" title="Soda bread">soda breads</a></i>. This method is commonly used to make <a href="/wiki/Muffin" title="Muffin">muffins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancake" title="Pancake">pancakes</a>, American-style <a href="/wiki/Biscuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Biscuits">biscuits</a>, and quick breads such as <a href="/wiki/Banana_bread" title="Banana bread">banana bread</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Yeast">Yeast</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker&#39;s yeast">Baker's yeast</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg/250px-Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg/500px-Compressed_fresh_yeast_-_1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1330" /></a><figcaption>Compressed fresh yeast</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many breads are leavened by <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>. The yeast most commonly used for leavening bread is <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i>, the same species used for brewing cereal-based alcoholic beverages. This yeast <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">ferments</a> some of the sugars producing <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Commercial bakers often leaven their dough with commercially produced <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker&#39;s yeast">baker's yeast</a>. Baker's yeast has the advantage of producing uniform, quick, and reliable results, because it is obtained from a <a href="/wiki/Pure_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Pure culture">pure culture</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BAC&#95;52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many artisan bakers produce their own yeast with a growth culture. If kept in the right conditions, it provides leavening for many years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-isbn0-387-38563-0-cite4&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-387-38563-0-cite4-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The baker's yeast and <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">sourdough</a> methods follow the same pattern. Water is mixed with flour, salt and the leavening agent. Other additions (spices, herbs, fats, seeds, fruit, etc.) are not needed to bake bread, but are often used. The mixed dough is then allowed to <a href="/wiki/Proofing_(baking_technique)" title="Proofing (baking technique)">rise</a> one or more times (a longer rising time results in more flavor, so bakers often "punch down" the dough and let it rise again), loaves are formed, and (after an optional final rising time) the bread is baked in an <a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">oven</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BAC&#95;52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many breads are made from a "<a href="/wiki/Straight_dough" title="Straight dough">straight dough</a>", which means that all of the ingredients are combined in one step, and the dough is baked after the rising time;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BAC&#95;52-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> others are made from a "<a href="/wiki/Pre-ferment" title="Pre-ferment">pre-ferment</a>" in which the leavening agent is combined with some of the flour and water a day or so ahead of baking and allowed to ferment overnight. On the day of baking, the rest of the ingredients are added, and the process continues as with straight dough. This produces a more flavorful bread with better texture. Many bakers see the starter method as a compromise between the reliable results of baker's yeast and the flavor and complexity of a longer fermentation. It also allows the baker to use only a minimal amount of baker's yeast, which was scarce and expensive when it first became available. Most yeasted pre-ferments fall into one of three categories: "<a href="/wiki/Poolish" class="mw-redirect" title="Poolish">poolish</a>" or "pouliche", a loose-textured mixture composed of roughly equal amounts of flour and water (by weight); "<a href="/wiki/Biga_(bread_baking)" title="Biga (bread baking)">biga</a>", a stiff mixture with a higher proportion of flour; and "pâte fermentée", which is a portion of dough reserved from a previous batch.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 126.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 124.66666666667px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Breaddough1.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Before first rising"><img alt="Before first rising" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Breaddough1.jpg/250px-Breaddough1.jpg" decoding="async" width="125" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Breaddough1.jpg/330px-Breaddough1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Breaddough1.jpg/500px-Breaddough1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="566" data-file-height="545" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 122px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 120px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Breaddough2.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="After first rising"><img alt="After first rising" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Breaddough2.jpg/250px-Breaddough2.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Breaddough2.jpg/330px-Breaddough2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Breaddough2.jpg/500px-Breaddough2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1942" data-file-height="1944" /></a></span></div>

		</li>

</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sourdough">Sourdough</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sourdough" title="Sourdough">Sourdough</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg/250px-Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg/500px-Sour_dough_loaves03.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a><figcaption>Sourdough loaves</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sourdough is a type of bread produced by a long fermentation of dough using naturally occurring yeasts and <a href="/wiki/Lactobacillus" title="Lactobacillus">lactobacilli</a>. It usually has a mildly sour taste because of the <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> produced during <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> by the lactobacilli. Longer fermented sourdoughs can also contain <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, the main non-water component of vinegar.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DavidsonSourdough&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonSourdough-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Sourdough breads are made with a sourdough starter. The starter cultivates yeast and lactobacilli in a mixture of flour and water, making use of the microorganisms already present on flour; it does not need any added yeast. A starter may be maintained indefinitely by regular additions of flour and water. Some bakers have starters many generations old, which are said to have a special taste or texture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DavidsonSourdough&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidsonSourdough-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At one time, all yeast-leavened breads were sourdoughs. Recently there has been a revival of sourdough bread in artisan bakeries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, peasant families throughout Europe baked on a fixed schedule, perhaps once a week. The starter was saved from the previous week's dough. The starter was mixed with the new ingredients, the dough was left to rise, and then a piece of it was saved to be the starter for next week's bread.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BAC&#95;52-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BAC-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Steam">Steam</h3></div>
<p>The rapid expansion of steam produced during baking leavens the bread, which is as simple as it is unpredictable.  Steam-leavening is unpredictable since the steam is not produced until the bread is baked. Steam leavening happens regardless of the raising agents (baking soda, yeast, baking powder, sour dough, beaten egg white) included in the mix. The leavening agent either contains air bubbles or generates carbon dioxide. The heat vaporises the water from the inner surface of the bubbles within the dough. The steam expands and makes the bread rise. This is the main factor in the rising of bread once it has been put in the oven.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> generation, on its own, is too small to account for the rise. Heat kills bacteria or yeast at an early stage, so the CO<sub>2</sub> generation is stopped.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Bacteria">Bacteria</h3></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Salt-rising_bread" title="Salt-rising bread">Salt-rising bread</a> does not use yeast. Instead, it is leavened by <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_perfringens" title="Clostridium perfringens">Clostridium perfringens</a></i>, one of the most common sources of food-borne illness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Aeration">Aeration</h3></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aerated_bread" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerated bread">Aerated bread</a> is leavened by carbon dioxide being forced into dough under pressure. From the mid-19th to mid-20th centuries, bread made this way was somewhat popular in the United Kingdom, made by the <a href="/wiki/Aerated_Bread_Company" title="Aerated Bread Company">Aerated Bread Company</a> and sold in its high-street <a href="/wiki/Aerated_Bread_Company#Tea_shops_and_early_women&#39;s_issues" title="Aerated Bread Company">tearooms</a>. The company was founded in 1862, and ceased independent operations in 1955.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-memoir&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-memoir-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Pressure-Vacuum mixer was later developed by the Flour Milling and Baking Research Association for the <a href="/wiki/Chorleywood_bread_process" title="Chorleywood bread process">Chorleywood bread process</a>. It manipulates the gas bubble size and optionally the composition of gases in the dough via the gas applied to the headspace.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cultural_significance">Cultural significance</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bread_in_culture" title="Bread in culture">Bread in culture</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg/250px-Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="238" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg/330px-Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg/500px-Rapid_Trident_2014_03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2112" data-file-height="2794" /></a><figcaption>A Ukrainian woman in national dress welcoming with <a href="/wiki/Bread_and_salt" title="Bread and salt">bread and salt</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bread_in_culture" title="Bread in culture">Bread plays an important role in many cultures</a> because of its historical role and contemporary importance. Bread is also <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_bread" title="Sacramental bread">significant in Christianity</a> as one of the elements (alongside <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in other religions including <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">Paganism</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In many <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>, bread is a <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a> for basic necessities and living conditions in general. For example, a "bread-winner" is a household's main economic contributor and has little to do with actual bread-provision. This is also seen in the phrase "putting bread on the table". The Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Juvenal" title="Juvenal">Juvenal</a> satirized superficial politicians and the public as caring only for "<i>panem et circenses</i>" (<a href="/wiki/Bread_and_circuses" title="Bread and circuses">bread and circuses</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russia</a> in 1917, the Bolsheviks promised "peace, land, and bread."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The term "<a href="/wiki/Breadbasket" title="Breadbasket">breadbasket</a>" denotes an agriculturally productive region. In parts of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern</a> Europe <a href="/wiki/Bread_and_salt" title="Bread and salt">bread and salt</a> is offered as a welcome to guests.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, life's basic necessities are often referred to as "roti, kapra aur makan" (bread, clothes, and house).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Words for bread, including "dough" and "bread" itself, are used in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>-speaking countries as <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a> for <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">money</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-etym&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etym-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A remarkable or revolutionary innovation may be called the best thing since "<a href="/wiki/Sliced_bread" title="Sliced bread">sliced bread</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The expression "to break bread with someone" means "to share a meal with someone".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The English word "lord" comes from the Anglo-Saxon <i>hlāfweard</i>, meaning "bread keeper."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bread is sometimes referred to as "the staff of life", although this term can refer to other staple foods in different cultures: the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> defines it as "bread (or similar staple food)".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-camb&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-camb-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Fraud">Fraud</h2></div>
<p>Bread has been subject to <a href="/wiki/Food_fraud" class="mw-redirect" title="Food fraud">food fraud</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adulteration" class="mw-redirect" title="Adulteration">adulteration</a> with fillers. In <a href="/wiki/Medieval_times" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval times">medieval times</a>, sand was used as a filler.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Russo-Ukrainian_war" title="Russo-Ukrainian war">Russo-Ukrainian war</a> has made sourcing <a href="/wiki/Wheat_flour" title="Wheat flour">wheat flour</a> more challenging and raised concerns of bread <a href="/wiki/Flour_fraud" class="mw-redirect" title="Flour fraud">flour fraud</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
